  "payload": "## ## Introduction It used to be common to hear cyber operations described as a cheaper form of strategic bombing or anonymous weapons of mass destruction.1 Thankfully this is changing. The empirical record of cyber conflict, now decades long, is not a good fit for warfighting and deterrence frameworks.2 Instead of catastrophic ‘cyberwar' we instead see interminable intrusions and disinformation campaigns in the ‘gray zone' between peace and war.3 Scholars have also highlighted many logical defects in assumptions about cyber warfare.4 Even the foundational metaphor of ‘cyberspace,' construed as a ‘warfighting domain' akin to the land, sea, air, and space environments, misconstrues computational networks as ‘virtual terrain' that must be controlled or defended.5 Yet if there is an emerging consensus about what cyber conflict is not, there is less agreement about what it is. Indeed, there is no shortage of metaphors available.6 While military imagery falls flat,7 better historical analogies can be found in crime,8 piracy,9 espionage,10 surveillance,11 disinformation,12 sabotage,13 covert action,14 proxy conflict,15 subversion,16 and counterintelligence.17 It is possible for any of these activities to have important consequences for international politics, even as -- if not because -- they are all something other than war. Yet they also have something else in common: all rely on deception, subterfuge, or ambiguity to gain a competitive advantage. Whether state‐based or non‐state, motivated by political interest or economic gain, most cyber actors are preoccupied with collecting and protecting secrets, at least to facilitate tactical intrusion if not to obfuscate attribution. In short, cyber operations look a lot like intelligence operations. As political scientist Joshua Rovner points out, ‘The ongoing competition in cyberspace is largely an intelligence contest. Although the technology is different, the underlying contest exhibits all the characteristics of traditional spy‐versus‐spy battles.'18 Computer security expert Matthew Monte states similarly that ‘Computer Network Exploitation is but the latest reincarnation of espionage.'19 Indeed, political actors have conducted espionage, subversion, and counterintelligence for millennia.20 Today these same activities can now be conducted with digital means, by a wider range of actors, against a wider range of targets, at historically unprecedented scope and scale.21 At the same time, the idea that cyber conflict is essentially an intelligence contest has received some pushback. There is debate over whether cybersecurity should be described as intelligence, special operations, or something altogether novel. Few would dispute that secrets can be collected through cyberspace, certainly in greater quantity if not quality. Yet some argue that cyber campaigns can accomplish other things besides. The difference in degree between cybersecurity and traditional intelligence or covert action may be so great as to be a difference in kind. Accordingly, U.S. Cyber Command (CYBERCOM) has articulated a new doctrine of ‘persistent engagement' to enable it to ‘defend forward' in renewed great power competition.\n\nCYBERCOM's actual operations -- penetrating adversarial networks for clandestine reconnaissance and discrete influence while protecting friendly networks from the same -- bear more than a passing resemblance to intelligence and counterintelligence activity, albeit at an industrial scale. Yet CYBERCOM has sound institutional and legal reasons to describe its activities as something other than intelligence. CYBERCOM emerged from the U.S. intelligence community, and still relies heavily upon it for technical assistance, but it is a brand-new military unified command with new authorities to conduct independent military operations. Indeed, the United States is in the process of building a major institutional investment on a category mistake. We thus find ourselves in a surreal situation in which the very organization that is pioneering intelligence operations at an unprecedented scale refuses to describe itself as an intelligence organization. Ceci n'est pas une pipe.\n\n---\n\n## ## Intelligence by other means What sort of thing is ‘intelligence' such that cyber conflict might be an instance of it? It is not very helpful to argue whether CYBERCOM is or is not conducting intelligence if we are unclear about what intelligence is. Most people would agree that cyber campaigns usually fall somewhere between peace and war, but does that make all of them intelligence campaigns? There are many types of unfriendly activities that fall short of war: intelligence, surely, but also crime, propaganda, secret diplomacy, and special operations. Do these all share enough in common -- secrecy, duplicity, deception, subversion, or whatever -- that we should lump them together as intelligence? We should be mindful here of Sartori's admonition against conceptual stretching: in ‘the Hegelian night in which all the cows look black... eventually the milkman is taken for a cow.'\n\nConsensus definitions for many important concepts like war, strategy, or politics remain elusive, but the concept of intelligence -- comprising a variety of practices that are furtive or transgressive by nature -- is especially fraught. Historically, many intelligence agencies have not only collected secrets but also have conducted covert action and counterintelligence, as well as taken on ancillary activities such as policing and running prisons. In World War II German intelligence (Abwehr) was divided into departments for espionage, subversion, and security. The KGB conducted ‘active measures' and internal security as well as espionage, and the CIA still has a paramilitary wing. The United States does make important legal distinctions between intelligence collection, covert action, and special operations, but this separation only emerged in the twentieth century; many other countries still do not make such bright distinctions. The modern distinctions in the United States government between intelligence collection and covert action, or between (Title 10) military operations and (Title 50) intelligence activities, or between CYBERCOM and the National Security Agency (NSA), might say more about the institutional context of cybersecurity and intelligence than the actual nature of either one.\nSecond, this secret activity can provide an information advantage in strategic competition. Intelligence collection (and analysis) can provide policymakers and commanders with threat warnings, assessments of developing trends, targeting opportunities, and feedback on progress. Covert action (including secret diplomacy) can offer options for disruption or persuasion without the political costs of overt bargaining. Counterintelligence (including operations security) attempts to prevent or redirect similar actions by hostile intelligence. Therefore, covert action, influence, and counterintelligence should be considered as essential intelligence practices, even if they are missing from the canonical intelligence cycle, and even if legal institutions distinguish them in practice. The essential difference between collection and influence is simply the direction that information flows through a secret channel. Intelligence organizations specialize in creating and protecting such channels. The information advantages created thereby can help decisionmakers to focus limited resources and avoid dangers. Weak actors can use intelligence to evade stronger competitors and identify their vulnerabilities, while strong actors can use it to conserve and amplify their strength. In short, intelligence enhances efficiency, by lowering the costs and risks of contested policy objectives.\nThird, Warner emphasizes that intelligence is sovereign activity, usually but not necessarily conducted by state intelligence agencies. In my opinion, this is the weakest of Warner's criteria. Not only is sovereignty a problematic concept, but also there are many actors other than states (such as firms, insurgencies, and criminal organizations) that can and do use deception to collect information and influence rivals. To quote the title of one of Warner's articles, ‘Intelligence is as intelligence does.' It is perhaps better to understand Warner's third criteria more broadly in terms of statecraft, which encompasses the political, economic, and military activities that any strategically autonomous actor conducts to survive and thrive in a competitive environment. In this sense even Al Qaeda and the Cosa Nostra engage in ‘statecraft' by building up military power, establishing alliances, and engaging in stratagems. They can use intelligence and skullduggery to improve the efficiency of these endeavors; indeed, they must do so to hide from and subvert stronger state security services. All three of Warner's criteria might be summarized compactly by defining intelligence as secret statecraft -- but not just by states.\n### Digital deception Are cyber operations, then, a form of secret statecraft? I believe that they are, first and foremost, because they rely on deception. Just as moles masquerade as trusted comrades, malware masquerades as reliable software. Both operate within the trusted institutions and practices that bind a community together, but they do a little extra on the side. Remote intruders do not, and cannot, use force directly. They can only break things or kill people indirectly by working within an established framework of software protocols to issue legitimate commands to an accessible and vulnerable host. Moreover, the majority of offensive operations in the wild aren't even destructive at all, but rather create reversible digital effects for the sake of espionage, disinformation, or harassment. Malware works by instructing machines to behave in ways that software developers and network operators do not intend. Hackers also use social engineering techniques -- phishing, waterholing, astroturfing, etc. -- to trick gullible humans into revealing credentials, opening doors, or amplifying signals. Likewise, any human spy or saboteur must employ ruses, deception, and confidence games to infiltrate an organization and harm a target.\nThe essence of deception, in human and technical operations alike, whether by simulation or dissimulation, for offense or defense, is the voluntary but unwitting cooperation of the deceived. A confidence man persuades his marks to act freely (or refrain from acting) against their own interests. Intelligence and cyber campaigns are essentially long cons that need to trick people and machines for an extended period of time to be successful. It is true that many aspects of cyber operations do not depend on deception. The most convincing ruses, after all, are built on the truth. A little lie is hard to spot in a mass of reasonable arguments. Likewise, cyber operations rely on the normal and reliable functioning of information systems, yet they also do a little extra on the side. Hackers exploit common rules and protocols for uncommon purposes. Even a very noisy cyber operation like a denial of service attack relies on tactical deception by using legitimate requests for connection to overwhelm the capacity of a server or communication channel. The deceptive or malicious use of rules is what often makes it possible to add new rules to disable any given attack, once it is understood. Everyday network operations and cyber defense in particular feature a lot of normal, non-deceptive computer usage. Yet even information assurance relies on deceptive tradecraft: encryption and obfuscation disguise sensitive operations and data as meaningless noise, while network surveillance attempts to detect suspicious threat activity without alerting the attacker. As with all forms of intelligence, offense must also play defense to avoid getting caught.\nImportantly, sustained deception usually relies on conspiracy. Most intelligence operations depend on intelligence organizations, not just lone spies. Likewise, cyber campaigns require an organizational network of planners, operators, and administrators, not just lone hackers. The more ambitious the deception and sensitive the target, the greater the need for a capable support organization. This is one reason that intelligence agencies in powerful states have important advantages over weaker powers and lone hackers, contrary to popular ideas about the asymmetric advantages of cyberspace.\nSecondly, digital deception can provide a competitive advantage. Cyber tools are particularly attractive for intelligence because the associated costs and risks seem so much lower relative to traditional forms of espionage and subversion. Interconnected networks are a bonanza for economic, political, and military espionage. Digital dependencies across organizations and societies create new covert action options, ranging from benign intelligence sharing with allies to more sporting disruption of critical infrastructure. All of these operations work by subverting the same information systems that are designed to enhance legitimate communication and coordination. By stealing data, injecting malicious instructions, or proffering disinformation, cyber operations aim to alter political or economic relationships in favor of one actor at another's expense.\nThirdly, state and nonstate actors alike can leverage computational networks for intelligence. To guard against network intrusions, information technology departments have been actively reinverting the art of counterintelligence. Private cybersecurity firms have come up with new concepts like ‘network security monitoring,' ‘active defense,' and ‘threat hunting' to describe best practices, but the essential techniques used to counter digital deceivers would be familiar to any spy. Some of this evolution has occurred naturally within intelligence agencies, for instance the NSA's transition from passive collection of signals intelligence (SIGINT) to active computer network exploitation, or through the migration of intelligence personnel to private security firms like Mandiant or CrowdStrike. Much of cybersecurity in the wild, however, can be interpreted as convergent evolution. The reason that dolphins look like sharks is because they are both marine predators subject to similar selection pressures in similar environments. The functional similarly of their forms results from similar competitive niches rather than any shared inheritance, given that the common genetic ancestor of sharks and dolphins is exceedingly remote. Likewise, cyber criminals, cybersecurity firms, and academic labs respond to many of the same functional imperatives for deception that have long motivated state intelligence agencies. To take just one example, the Citizen Lab at the University of Toronto studies digital surveillance targeting journalists, activists, and scholars; in doing so it has itself taken on many of the trappings of the spy agencies it investigates, to include physical access controls, data restrictions, and regular security audits. Similarly, security researchers at the University of California San Diego reinvented the counterintelligence wheel to elicit information from scammers so that they could study people who did not want to be studied.\nTo sum up, cyber operations use deceptive tradecraft for political statecraft, but not only by states. Cyber exploitation, attack, and defense are digital reincarnations of classic collection, influence, and counterintelligence practice (see Table 1). To be sure, cyber conflict involves countless nonstate actors as agents, intermediaries, and targets, and cyber campaigns unfold at unprecedented scope and scale. Nevertheless, cybersecurity is still intelligence, digitized and supersized.\n### The problem of scale Although cybersecurity appears to be intelligence by Michael Warner's criteria, he argues that it has evolved into something other than intelligence: ‘the technology of cyberspace seems to be producing something unexpected: operations and effects that resemble covert actions but are much larger in their scale and reach.' Traditional conspiracies and espionage rings were usually limited in size and scale. The more people involved and the more ambitious their reach, the more likely they were to be detected and rolled up. Since secrecy is necessary to intelligence, the risk of compromise has always been a limiting factor on the scale of intelligence. However, Warner argues, ‘cyberspace seems to have fixed covert action's problem of scale.' He is on to something important. Ubiquitous networked technology appears to make collection and influence cheaper, easier, and less risky. Weaker states and nonstate actors now have opportunities to do things that once required a well-resourced state intelligence agency. Lower operational costs enable actors to dramatically scale up their activities by infiltrating thousands of machines in a single operation or tailoring messages for millions of different users. This also means that many actors previously disinterested in intelligence now have incentives to take notice.\nFor similar reasons, political scientists Richard Harknett and Max Smeets also resist the reduction of cybersecurity to intelligence. Instead they emphasize that cyber campaigns ‘take place over time, to achieve a cumulative outcome leading to strategic advantage.' They argue that this new potential is determined by the networked structure of cyberspace: ‘The technological nature of the environment and the inherent dynamic that follows from the technology suggests that the opportunities for seeking initiative is exponentially higher in cyberspace across both time and geographical space.' They agree that digital technology has expanded the scope or variety of intelligence activity, but they argue that ‘an expansion in scope of operations has been accompanied by an expansion in scale and, when taken together, is resulting in a difference in kind and not merely degree.'\n[Table 1.] New cyber variations on classic intelligence themes.\n[ There are two problems with this argument. First is that the modern intelligence enterprise is built on a dramatic technological expansion of possibilities for espionage. The telegraphic and wireless revolutions over a century ago vastly expanded the speed, variety, volume, and vulnerability of all kinds of communications, and thus SIGINT. Complementary innovation in aviation, ships, submarines, satellites, and sensors gave birth to entirely new technical collection disciplines. Through them intelligence scaled up dramatically to reconnoiter and contest the entire planet. The great SIGINT campaigns of the Second World War exemplified cumulative strategic action, as the Allies leveraged persistent and pervasive penetration of enemy networks to provide information advantages to policymakers and commanders. Throughout the Cold War, as Warner has so ably demonstrated, the superpowers spied not only on each other, but also their economic industries and domestic populations. If intelligence mattered at all in this titanic contest, it mattered as a sustained ‘cumulative outcome leading to strategic advantage,' to borrow the words that Harknett and Smeets use to describe the supposedly unique feature of cyber campaigns. It is telling that the central empirical case in their article is the competitive advantage China has allegedly gained through cyber espionage. Of course, the KGB ‘Line X' program attempted something similar during the Cold War, an effort that was breathtaking in scale by the standards of the day. The increases in the scope and scale of intelligence enabled by the internet revolution thus seem very much like a difference in degree rather than kind.\nThe second problem is that the economic feature that enables cyber operations at scale -- reduced transaction cost -- is entirely consistent with the political logic of intelligence. The cyber variations of intelligence practice in can indeed be conducted at unprecedented scale at relatively low cost and risk. Malicious operators can build on the same software components developed and communication networks connected for legitimate reasons, with reduced exposure to detection and retaliation. According to Harknett and Smeets, ‘the fundamental nature of cyberspace rests on a structure of interconnectedness and a condition of constant contact. Once recognised, that structural environment requires us to study cyber means not as enablers of war, although they can be, but more critically as the strategic alternative to it.' Yet this simply recapitulates the classic efficiency logic of intelligence. Intelligence has always been parasitic on the existing communication networks or cultural practices of the host society. By stealthily extracting or injecting information into an extant organizational system, a political actor attempts to do a little bit more with a little bit less. War is costly in the extreme, and diplomatic negotiation or coercion can be costly as well. Intelligence and covert action aim to realize some of their benefits without paying all of their costs. This includes using military intelligence reduce the costs of war by fighting a smarter, more limited, or shorter conflict. Cyber operations may make intelligence cheaper and less risky, but the political motivation of secret statecraft has always been the reduction of costs and risks, even as unintended consequences often end up inadvertently increasing them. Digital espionage and disinformation may indeed be much more efficient than reliance on human spies for collection and influence at scale, but human spies themselves are more efficient than reliance on shear mass and raw power to overcome uncertainty. If intelligence provides an information advantage, then cyber operations just provide more information.\nIt is important, however, not to conflate the enhanced efficiency of cyber operations with enhanced effectiveness in political competition. Deception cuts both ways in an intelligence contest. Indeed, scale is an important condition that mitigates several dynamics that are often ascribed categorically to the intrinsic nature of cyberspace, but which are actually grounded in the political dynamics of deception. For instance, the so-called attribution problem is hardest when there are many potential culprits and the victim is not willing or able to invest in a thorough investigation. When there are only one or two actors with the requisite capability to pull off an attack, and a mass of damning circumstantial context beyond the tactical cyber forensics, then political attribution is relatively easier. Offense has the advantage, similarly, and deterrence tends to fail, when engineering an intrusion is simpler and the likelihood of costly retaliation is low. Attackers tend to be more circumspect against technically complex and politically sensitive targets. The variable feasibility of deterrence and the value of continuing interconnectivity combine to drive the intelligence and cyber operations toward the lower end of the conflict spectrum. Finally, classic trade-offs between using and losing intelligence -- described in terms of ‘intelligence gains and losses' in military circles and the ‘vulnerabilities equity problem' in cyber circles -- can also be expected to become more pronounced at scale.\nTechnological scale does not differentiate cyber campaigns from intelligence. If anything, scale just makes the comparison that much more apt. Information technology tends amplify the efficiency gains of secret statecraft. The real problem may not be that international relations scholars have failed to understand the novelties of cyberspace, but rather that their discipline has traditionally neglected the role of intelligence in international politics. The increasing scope and scale of intelligence in the twenty first century makes it impossible to ignore it any longer. The recognition that there can be cumulative strategic options short of war should be recognized as part of the growing scholarly interest in secret statecraft in general, not an alternative to it.",
  "summary_log": "---LOG_SUMMARY_START---\ndoc_status:SUCCESS\nsections_raw:6\nsections_clean:6\nintro:FOUND\nconclusion:FOUND\npredefined_sections:## The bureaucratic domain Nevertheless, there may be other reasons to resist describing cybersecurity as an intelligence contest. Warner may be right that the increasing scale of covert activities is overwhelming the ability of traditional intelligence institutions to handle them. Moreover, intelligence agencies in democratic states might be expected to become embroiled in civil liberties controversies as civilian technology firms and other civil society actors become increasingly implicated in intelligence contests (as actors, intermediaries, and targets). Similarly, military organizations can be expected to struggle with integrating intelligence at scale as operations of all types become more dependent on reconnaissance, targeting, and combat assessment. Importantly, these challenges have more to do with the administrative and legal implementation of intelligence than questions about its nature. Finally, if the problems and opportunities inherent in scaling up intelligence do end up encouraging organizational innovation, then it is also reasonable to expect political entrepreneurs to attempt to distinguish themselves from intelligence incumbents. Something like this appears to be happening in the United States.|## Conclusion There is no easy solution for the problems of intelligence at scale. Intelligence contests are battles of wits wherein adversaries feign cooperation in ruthless competition. Because all sides of an intelligence contest can employ deception, increasingly via civil society, the results of these contests are often ambiguous, frustrating, and disappointing. Often the only thing that cumulates through an intelligence campaign is friction. Such is the nature of intelligence. Cyber campaigns are sure to be full of friction and disappointment as well.\nextra_sections:None\npayload_tokens_before:9385\npayload_tokens_after:8892\ndropped_section:## Conclusion There is no easy solution for the problems of intelligence at scale. Intelligence contests are battles of wits wherein adversaries feign cooperation in ruthless competition. Because all sides of an intelligence contest can employ deception, increasingly via civil society, the results of these contests are often ambiguous, frustrating, and disappointing. Often the only thing that cumulates through an intelligence campaign is friction. Such is the nature of intelligence. Cyber campaigns are sure to be full of friction and disappointment as well.\nadded_section:None\n---LOG_SUMMARY_END---",
